<p>The <b>giraffe</b> (<i>Giraffa</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> of African <a href="/wiki/Even-toed_ungulate" title="Even-toed ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a> <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Largest_mammals#Even-toed_Ungulates_.28Artiodactyla.29" title="Largest mammals">tallest</a> living terrestrial animals and the largest <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a>. The genus consists of eleven species including <i>Giraffa camelopardalis</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type species</a>. Seven of these species are extinct, prehistoric species known from fossils, while four are still extant. Whereas <i>Giraffa</i> once was thought to contain a single extant species with numerous subspecies, research into the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_DNA" title="Nuclear DNA">nuclear DNA</a> of <i>Giraffa</i> has revealed four to six distinct extant species. The four-species taxonomic classification has the genus <i>Giraffa</i> composed of the species <i><a href="/wiki/Southern_giraffe" title="Southern giraffe">Giraffa giraffa</a></i> (southern giraffe), <i><a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Giraffa tippelskirchi</a></i> (Masai giraffe), <i><a href="/wiki/Reticulated_giraffe" title="Reticulated giraffe">Giraffa reticulata</a></i> (reticulated giraffe) and <i><a href="/wiki/Northern_giraffe" title="Northern giraffe">Giraffa camelopardalis</a></i> (northern giraffe).</p>
<p>The giraffe's chief distinguishing characteristics are its extremely long neck and legs, its horn-like <a href="/wiki/Ossicone" title="Ossicone">ossicones</a>, and its distinctive coat patterns. It is classified under the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Giraffidae" title="Giraffidae">Giraffidae</a>, along with its closest extant relative, the <a href="/wiki/Okapi" title="Okapi">okapi</a>. Each of the four species is distinguished by its coat patterns and genetics. Its scattered range extends from <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> in the north to <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> in the south, and from <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> in the west to <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> in the east. Giraffes usually inhabit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">savannahs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a>. Their food source is leaves, fruits and flowers of woody plants, primarily <a href="/wiki/Acacieae" title="Acacieae">acacia</a> species, which they browse at heights most other herbivores cannot reach. Giraffes may be preyed on by <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">spotted hyenas</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_wild_dog" title="African wild dog">African wild dogs</a>. Giraffes live in herds of related females and their offspring, or bachelor herds of unrelated adult males, but are gregarious and may gather in large aggregations. Males establish social hierarchies through "necking", which are combat bouts where the neck is used as a weapon. <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)" title="Dominance (ethology)">Dominant</a> males gain mating access to females, which bear the sole responsibility for raising the young.</p>
<p>The giraffe has intrigued various cultures, both ancient and modern, for its peculiar appearance, and has often been featured in paintings, books, and cartoons. It is classified by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> as <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a> to extinction, and has been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extirpated" title="Extirpated">extirpated</a> from many parts of its former range. Giraffes are still found in numerous <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Game_reserve" title="Game reserve">game reserves</a> but estimations as of 2016 indicate that there are approximately 97,500 members of <i>Giraffa</i> in the wild, with around 1,144 in captivity.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The name "giraffe" has its earliest known origins in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> word <i>zarāfah</i> (زرافة),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">[2]</a></sup> perhaps borrowed from the animal's <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> name <i>geri</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The Arab name is translated as "fast-walker".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-4">[4]</a></sup> There were several <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> spellings, such as <i>jarraf</i>, <i>ziraph</i>, and <i>gerfauntz</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">[2]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> form <i>giraffa</i> arose in the 1590s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">[2]</a></sup> The modern English form developed around 1600 from the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>girafe</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">[2]</a></sup> "Camelopard" is an archaic English name for the giraffe deriving from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> for camel and leopard, referring to its <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a>-like shape and its <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopard</a>-like colouring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h2>
<p>Living giraffes were originally classified as one species by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1758. He gave it the binomial name <i><a href="/wiki/Cervus" title="Cervus">Cervus</a> camelopardalis</i>. <a href="/wiki/Morten_Thrane_Br%C3%BCnnich" title="Morten Thrane Brünnich">Morten Thrane Brünnich</a> classified the genus <i>Giraffa</i> in 1772.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> The species name <i>camelopardalis</i> is from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffidcomparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1544" data-file-width="1115" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Giraffidcomparison.jpg/200px-Giraffidcomparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Giraffidcomparison.jpg/300px-Giraffidcomparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Giraffidcomparison.jpg/400px-Giraffidcomparison.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffidcomparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The extinct giraffid <i><a href="/wiki/Samotherium" title="Samotherium">Samotherium</a></i> (middle) in comparison with the okapi (below) and giraffe. The anatomy of <i>Samotherium</i> appears to have shown a transition to a giraffe-like neck.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The giraffe belongs to the suborder <a href="/wiki/Ruminantia" title="Ruminantia">Ruminantia</a>. Many ruminants have been described from the mid-<a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. The earliest ruminants date back to about 50 million years ago. The Rumanantia arose from early artiodactyls, which experienced adaptive radiation in the middle Eocene period that led to adaptations related to herbivory. The oldest known artiodactyl lived in Holarctic of Pangea, which then occupied regions in North America, Europe, and Asia around 60–55 million years ago in the early Eucene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The ecological conditions during this period may have helped them to disperse fast. The giraffe is one of only two living genera of the family <a href="/wiki/Giraffidae" title="Giraffidae">Giraffidae</a>, the other being the <a href="/wiki/Okapi" title="Okapi">okapi</a>. The family was once much more extensive, with over 10 fossil <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> described. Their closest known relatives are the extinct deer-like <a href="/wiki/Climacoceratidae" title="Climacoceratidae">climacocerids</a>. They, together with the family <a href="/wiki/Antilocapridae" title="Antilocapridae">Antilocapridae</a> (whose only extant species is the <a href="/wiki/Pronghorn" title="Pronghorn">pronghorn</a>), belong to the superfamily <a href="/wiki/Giraffoidea" title="Giraffoidea">Giraffoidea</a>. These animals may have evolved from the extinct family <a href="/wiki/Palaeomerycidae" title="Palaeomerycidae">Palaeomerycidae</a> which might also have been the ancestor of <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The elongation of the neck appears to have started early in the giraffe <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)" title="Lineage (evolution)">lineage</a>. Comparisons between giraffes and their ancient relatives suggest that vertebrae close to the skull lengthened earlier, followed by lengthening of vertebrae further down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danowitz2015_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Danowitz2015-11">[11]</a></sup> One early giraffid ancestor was <i><a href="/wiki/Canthumeryx" title="Canthumeryx">Canthumeryx</a></i> which has been dated variously to have lived 25–20 million years ago (mya), 17–15 mya or 18–14.3 mya and whose deposits have been found in Libya. This animal was medium-sized, slender and antelope-like. <i><a href="/wiki/Giraffokeryx" title="Giraffokeryx">Giraffokeryx</a></i> appeared 15 mya in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and resembled an okapi or a small giraffe, and had a longer neck and similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ossicones" title="Ossicones">ossicones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup> <i>Giraffokeryx</i> may have shared a clade with more massively built giraffids like <i><a href="/wiki/Sivatherium" title="Sivatherium">Sivatherium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bramatherium" title="Bramatherium">Bramatherium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danowitz2015_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Danowitz2015-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Giraffids like <i><a href="/wiki/Palaeotragus" title="Palaeotragus">Palaeotragus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shansitherium" title="Shansitherium">Shansitherium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Samotherium" title="Samotherium">Samotherium</a></i> appeared 14 mya and lived throughout Africa and Eurasia. These animals had bare ossicones and small cranial sinuses and were longer with broader skulls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danowitz2015_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Danowitz2015-11">[11]</a></sup> <i>Paleotragus</i> resembled the okapi and may have been its ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup> Others find that the okapi lineage diverged earlier, before <i>Giraffokeryx</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danowitz2015_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Danowitz2015-11">[11]</a></sup> <i>Samotherium</i> was a particularly important <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">transitional fossil</a> in the giraffe lineage as its cervical vertebrae was intermediate in length and structure between a modern giraffe and an okapi, and was more vertical than the okapi's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Bohlinia" title="Bohlinia">Bohlinia</a></i>, which first appeared in southeastern Europe and lived 9–7 mya was likely a direct ancestor of the giraffe. <i>Bohlinia</i> closely resembled modern giraffes, having a long neck and legs and similar ossicones and dentition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_jumae.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="1903" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Giraffa_jumae.JPG/250px-Giraffa_jumae.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Giraffa_jumae.JPG/375px-Giraffa_jumae.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Giraffa_jumae.JPG/500px-Giraffa_jumae.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_jumae.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fossil of <i>Giraffa jumae</i> at <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum, London</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Bohlinia</i> entered China and northern India in response to climate change. From there, the genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giraffa" title="Giraffa">Giraffa</a></i> evolved and, around 7 mya, entered Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TPBDBGiraffa_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-TPBDBGiraffa-13">[13]</a></sup> Further climate changes caused the extinction of the Asian giraffes, while the African giraffes survived and radiated into several new species. Living giraffes appear to have arisen around 1 mya in eastern Africa during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup> Some biologists suggest the modern giraffes descended from <i><a href="/wiki/Giraffa_jumae" title="Giraffa jumae">G. jumae</a></i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup> others find <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Giraffa_gracilis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Giraffa gracilis (page does not exist)">G. gracilis</a></i> a more likely candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup> <i>G. jumae</i> was larger and more heavily built while <i>G. gracilis</i> was smaller and more lightly built. The main driver for the evolution of the giraffes is believed to have been the changes from extensive forests to more open habitats, which began 8 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup> During this time, tropical plants disappeared and were replaced by arid C4 plants, and a dry savannah emerged across eastern and northern Africa and western India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Some researchers have hypothesised that this new habitat coupled with a different diet, including acacia species, may have exposed giraffe ancestors to toxins that caused higher mutation rates and a higher rate of evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bada_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-bada-17">[17]</a></sup> The coat patterns of modern giraffes may also have coincided with these habitat changes. Asian giraffes are hypothesised to have had more okapi-like colourations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a> believed the giraffe's long neck was an "acquired characteristic", developed as generations of ancestral giraffes strove to reach the leaves of tall trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup> This theory was eventually rejected, and scientists now believe the giraffe's neck arose through Darwinian <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>—that ancestral giraffes with long necks thereby had a competitive feeding advantage (competing browsers hypothesis)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> that better enabled them to survive and reproduce to pass on their genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The giraffe <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> is around 2.9 billion <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> in length compared to the 3.3 billion base pairs of the okapi. Of the proteins in giraffe and okapi genes, 19.4% are identical. The two species are equally distantly related to cattle, suggesting the giraffe's unique characteristics are not because of faster of evolution. The <a href="/wiki/Divergent_evolution" title="Divergent evolution">divergence</a> of giraffe and okapi lineages dates to around 11.5 mya. A small group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulatory_gene" title="Regulatory gene">regulatory genes</a> in the giraffe appear to be responsible for the animal's stature and associated circulatory adaptations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genome_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genome-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_and_subspecies">Species and subspecies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="606" data-file-width="936" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png/300px-Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png/450px-Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png/600px-Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genetic_subdivision_in_the_giraffe_based_on_mitochondrial_DNA_sequences.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Approximate geographic ranges, fur patterns, and <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> relationships between some giraffe subspecies based on <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> sequences. Colored dots on the map represent sampling localities. The phylogenetic tree is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maximum-likelihood" title="Maximum-likelihood">maximum-likelihood</a> <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree#Special_tree_types" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogram</a> based on samples from 266 giraffes. Asterisks along branches correspond to <a href="/wiki/Clade#Definitions" title="Clade">node</a> values of more than 90% <a href="/wiki/Resampling_(statistics)" title="Resampling (statistics)">bootstrap</a> support. Stars at branch tips identify <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplotype" title="Haplotype">haplotypes</a> found in Maasai and reticulated giraffes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeneticStructure_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-GeneticStructure-21">[21]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A 2007 study on the genetics of <i>Giraffa</i>, which were thought to combine to compose a single giraffe species suggested they were six species: the West African, Rothschild's, reticulated, Masai, Angolan, and South African giraffe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeneticStructure_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-GeneticStructure-21">[21]</a></sup> The study deduced from <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_DNA" title="Nuclear DNA">nuclear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA) that giraffes from these populations are <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_isolation" title="Reproductive isolation">reproductively isolated</a> and rarely interbreed, though no natural obstacles block their mutual access. This includes adjacent populations of Rothschild's, reticulated, and Masai giraffes. The Masai giraffe was also suggested to consist of possibly two species separated by the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">Rift Valley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeneticStructure_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-GeneticStructure-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reticulated and Masai giraffes have the highest mtDNA diversity, which is consistent with giraffes originating in eastern Africa. Populations further north are more closely related to the former, while those to the south are more related to the latter. Giraffes appear to select mates of the same coat type, which are imprinted on them as calves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeneticStructure_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-GeneticStructure-21">[21]</a></sup> The implications of these findings for the conservation of giraffes were summarised by David Brown, lead author of the study, who told <a href="/wiki/BBC_News" title="BBC News">BBC News</a>: "Lumping all giraffes into one species obscures the reality that some kinds of giraffe are on the brink. Some of these populations number only a few hundred individuals and need immediate protection."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2016 study confirmed that living giraffes consist of multiple species. The researchers supported the existence of four species, which have not exchanged genetic information between each other for 1 million to 2 million years. Those four species are the <a href="/wiki/Northern_giraffe" title="Northern giraffe">northern giraffe</a> (<i>Giraffa camelopardalis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Southern_giraffe" title="Southern giraffe">southern giraffe</a> (<i>Giraffa giraffa</i>), <a href="/wiki/Reticulated_giraffe" title="Reticulated giraffe">reticulated giraffe</a> (<i>Giraffa reticulata</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> (<i>Giraffa tippelskirchi</i>). Both southern and northern giraffe have two and three distinct subspecies respectively; <a href="/wiki/Angolan_giraffe" title="Angolan giraffe">Angolan giraffe</a> (<i>G. g. angolensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/South_African_giraffe" title="South African giraffe">South African giraffe</a> (<i>G. g. giraffa</i>) for southern giraffe, and the <a href="/wiki/Kordofan_giraffe" title="Kordofan giraffe">Kordofan giraffe</a> (<i>G. c. antiquorum</i>), <a href="/wiki/Nubian_giraffe" title="Nubian giraffe">Nubian giraffe</a> (<i>G. c. camelopardalis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/West_African_giraffe" title="West African giraffe">West African giraffe</a> (<i>G. c. peralta</i>) for northern giraffe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Up to four species, and five subspecies, of giraffe are recognized (with population estimates as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Giraffe&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>). Two former subspecies (Rothschild's giraffe and Rhodesian giraffe) are also included. There are an estimated 89,710 individuals of <i>Giraffa</i> in the wild, with 1,144 currently in captivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Species and subspecies of giraffe</caption>
<tr>
<th>Species</th>
<th>Subspecies</th>
<th>Description</th>
<th>Image</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="4"><b><a href="/wiki/Northern_giraffe" title="Northern giraffe">Northern giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. camelopardalis</i>)</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Kordofan_giraffe" title="Kordofan giraffe">Kordofan giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. c. antiquorum</i>)</td>
<td>The Kordofan giraffe has a distribution which includes southern <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, and north-eastern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">DR Congo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> Populations in Cameroon were formerly included in <i>G. c. peralta</i>, but this was incorrect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WestAfricaGiraffe_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-WestAfricaGiraffe-26">[26]</a></sup> Compared to the Nubian giraffe, this subspecies has smaller and more irregular spotting patterns. Its spots may be found below the hocks and the insides of the legs. A median lump is present in males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:51–52</sup> Some 2,000 are believed to remain in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> Considerable confusion has existed over the status of this subspecies and <i>G. c. peralta</i> in zoos. In 2007, all alleged <i>G. c. peralta</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_zoos#Europe" title="List of zoos">European zoos</a> were shown to be, in fact, <i>G. c. antiquorum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WestAfricaGiraffe_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-WestAfricaGiraffe-26">[26]</a></sup> With this correction, about 65 are kept in zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup> The formerly recognised subspecies <i>G. c. congoesis</i> is now considered part of Kordofan subspecies.</td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_camelopardalis_antiquorum_(Vincennes_Zoo)_2.jpg"><img alt="Giraffa camelopardalis antiquorum (Vincennes Zoo) 2.jpg" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Giraffa_camelopardalis_antiquorum_%28Vincennes_Zoo%29_2.jpg/150px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_antiquorum_%28Vincennes_Zoo%29_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Giraffa_camelopardalis_antiquorum_%28Vincennes_Zoo%29_2.jpg/225px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_antiquorum_%28Vincennes_Zoo%29_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Giraffa_camelopardalis_antiquorum_%28Vincennes_Zoo%29_2.jpg/300px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_antiquorum_%28Vincennes_Zoo%29_2.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Nubian_giraffe" title="Nubian giraffe">Nubian giraffe</a></b>, (<i>G. c. camelopardalis</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup></td>
<td>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nominate_subspecies" title="Nominate subspecies">nominate subspecies</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_giraffe" title="Northern giraffe">northern giraffe</a>, is found in eastern <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> and south-western <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> in addition to <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> It has sharply defined chestnut-coloured spots surrounded by mostly white lines, while undersides lack spotting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> The median lump is particularly developed in the male.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:51</sup> Around 2,150 are thought to remain in the wild, with 1,500 individuals belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Rothschild%27s_giraffe" title="Rothschild's giraffe">Rothschild</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecotype" title="Ecotype">ecotype</a>. Due to the addition of <a href="/wiki/Rothschild%27s_giraffe" title="Rothschild's giraffe">Rothschild's giraffe</a> to the Nubian subspecies, the Nubian giraffe is very common in captivity, although the original phenotype is rare- a group is kept at <a href="/wiki/Al_Ain_Zoo" title="Al Ain Zoo">Al Ain Zoo</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al_Ain_exhibits_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Al_Ain_exhibits-29">[29]</a></sup> In 2003, this group numbered 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al_Ain_Zoo_Giraffe.JPG"><img alt="Al Ain Zoo Giraffe.JPG" data-file-height="411" data-file-width="614" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Al_Ain_Zoo_Giraffe.JPG/150px-Al_Ain_Zoo_Giraffe.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Al_Ain_Zoo_Giraffe.JPG/225px-Al_Ain_Zoo_Giraffe.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Al_Ain_Zoo_Giraffe.JPG/300px-Al_Ain_Zoo_Giraffe.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/West_African_giraffe" title="West African giraffe">West African giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. c. peralta</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup> also known as <b><a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> giraffe</b> or <b><a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigerian</a> giraffe</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNperalta_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNperalta-31">[31]</a></sup></td>
<td>The West African giraffe is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic">endemic</a> to south-western Niger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucn_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> This animal has a lighter pelage than other subspecies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:322</sup> with red lobe-shaped blotches that reach below the hocks. The ossicones are more erect than in other subspecies and males have well-developed median lumps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:52–53</sup> It is the most endangered subspecies within <i>Giraffa</i>, with 400 individuals remaining in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> Giraffes in Cameroon were formerly believed to belong to this subspecies, but are actually <i>G. c. antiquorum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WestAfricaGiraffe_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-WestAfricaGiraffe-26">[26]</a></sup> This error resulted in some confusion over its status in zoos, but in 2007, it was established that all "<i>G. c. peralta</i>" kept in European zoos actually are <i>G. c. antiquorum</i>. The same 2007 study found that The West African giraffe was more closely related to the Rothchild's giraffe than the Kordofan and its ancestor may have migrated from eastern to northern Africa and then to its current range with the development of the Sahara Desert. At its largest, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a> may have acted as a barrier between West African and Kordofan giraffes during the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> (before 5000 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WestAfricaGiraffe_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-WestAfricaGiraffe-26">[26]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_koure_niger_2006.jpg"><img alt="Giraffe koure niger 2006.jpg" data-file-height="659" data-file-width="990" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Giraffe_koure_niger_2006.jpg/150px-Giraffe_koure_niger_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Giraffe_koure_niger_2006.jpg/225px-Giraffe_koure_niger_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Giraffe_koure_niger_2006.jpg/300px-Giraffe_koure_niger_2006.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Rothschild%27s_giraffe" title="Rothschild's giraffe">Rothschild's giraffe</a></b> ("<i>G. c. rothschildi</i>", after <a href="/wiki/Walter_Rothschild,_2nd_Baron_Rothschild" title="Walter Rothschild, 2nd Baron Rothschild">Walter Rothschild</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup> also known as <b><a href="/wiki/Lake_Baringo" title="Lake Baringo">Baringo</a> giraffe</b> or <b><a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Ugandan</a> giraffe</b></td>
<td>The Rothschild's giraffe is now considered an <a href="/wiki/Ecotype" title="Ecotype">ecotype</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_giraffe" title="Northern giraffe">northern giraffe</a> (<i>Giraffa camelopardalis</i>). Its range includes parts of Uganda and Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucn_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> Its presence in South Sudan is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNrothschildi_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNrothschildi-33">[33]</a></sup> This giraffe has large dark patches that usually have complete margins, but may also have sharp edges. The dark spots may also have paler radiating lines or streaks within them. Spotting does not often reach below the hocks and almost never to the hooves. This ecotype may also develop five "horns".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:53</sup> Around 1,500 individuals believed to remain in the wild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> and more than 450 are kept in zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup> According to genetic analysis circa September 2016, it is <a href="/wiki/Biological_specificity" title="Biological specificity">conspecific</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_giraffe" title="Nubian giraffe">Nubian giraffe</a> (<i>G. c. camelopardalis</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_camelopardalis_rothschildi_1.jpg"><img alt="Giraffa camelopardalis rothschildi 1.jpg" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Giraffa_camelopardalis_rothschildi_1.jpg/150px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_rothschildi_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Giraffa_camelopardalis_rothschildi_1.jpg/225px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_rothschildi_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Giraffa_camelopardalis_rothschildi_1.jpg/300px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_rothschildi_1.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2"><b><a href="/wiki/Reticulated_giraffe" title="Reticulated giraffe">Reticulated giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. reticulata</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup> also known as <b>Somali giraffe</b></td>
<td>The reticulated giraffe is native to north-eastern <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, southern Ethiopia, and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> Its distinctive coat pattern consists of sharp-edged, reddish brown polygonal patches divided by a network of thin white lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> Spots may or may not extend below the hocks, and a median lump is present in males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:53</sup> An estimated 8,660 individuals remain in the wild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> and based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Species_Information_System" title="International Species Information System">International Species Information System</a> records, more than 450 are kept in zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_Giraffes.PNG"><img alt="Two Giraffes.PNG" data-file-height="1578" data-file-width="1332" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Two_Giraffes.PNG/150px-Two_Giraffes.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Two_Giraffes.PNG/225px-Two_Giraffes.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Two_Giraffes.PNG/300px-Two_Giraffes.PNG 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2"><b><a href="/wiki/Southern_giraffe" title="Southern giraffe">Southern giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. giraffa</i>)</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Angolan_giraffe" title="Angolan giraffe">Angolan giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. g. angolensis</i>), also known as <b>Namibian giraffe</b></td>
<td>The Angolan giraffe is found in northern <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, south-western <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, and western <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-7"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> A 2009 genetic study on this subspecies suggested the northern <a href="/wiki/Namib_Desert" title="Namib Desert">Namib Desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Etosha_National_Park" title="Etosha National Park">Etosha National Park</a> populations form a separate subspecies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This subspecies has large brown blotches with edges that are either somewhat notched or have angular extensions. The spotting pattern extends throughout the legs but not the upper part of the face. The neck and rump patches tend to be fairly small. The subspecies also has a white ear patch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:51</sup> An estimated 13,000 animals are estimated to remain in the wild;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-8"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> and about 20 are kept in zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_camelopardalis_-Zambia-8.jpg"><img alt="Giraffa camelopardalis -Zambia-8.jpg" data-file-height="2352" data-file-width="2112" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Giraffa_camelopardalis_-Zambia-8.jpg/150px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_-Zambia-8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Giraffa_camelopardalis_-Zambia-8.jpg/225px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_-Zambia-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Giraffa_camelopardalis_-Zambia-8.jpg/300px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_-Zambia-8.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/South_African_giraffe" title="South African giraffe">South African giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. g. giraffa</i>)</td>
<td>The South African giraffe is found in northern <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, southern Botswana, southern Zimbabwe, and south-western <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-8"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> It has dark, somewhat rounded patches "with some fine projections" on a tawny background colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-9"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> The spots extend down the legs and get smaller. The median lump of males is less developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:52</sup> A maximum of 31,500 are estimated to remain in the wild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-9"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> and around 45 are kept in zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_standing.jpg"><img alt="Giraffe standing.jpg" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="1712" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Giraffe_standing.jpg/150px-Giraffe_standing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Giraffe_standing.jpg/225px-Giraffe_standing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Giraffe_standing.jpg/300px-Giraffe_standing.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2"><b><a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. tippelskirchi</i>)</td>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a></b> (<i>G. tippelskirchi</i>), also known as <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilimanjaro" title="Kilimanjaro">Kilimanjaro</a> giraffe</b></td>
<td>The Masai giraffe can be found in central and southern Kenya and in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-10"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> It has distinctive, irregular, jagged, star-like blotches which extend to the hooves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-11"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> A median lump is usually present in males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:54</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> A total of 32,550 are thought to remain in the wild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-10"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> (including the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_giraffe" title="Rhodesian giraffe">Rhodesian</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecotype" title="Ecotype">ecotype</a>) and about 100 are kept in zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG"><img alt="GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG" data-file-height="3940" data-file-width="2130" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG/150px-GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG/225px-GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG/300px-GiraffaCamelopardalisTippelskirchi-Masaai-Mara.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_giraffe" title="Rhodesian giraffe">Rhodesian giraffe</a></b> ("<i>G. c. thornicrofti</i>", after <a href="/wiki/Harry_Scott_Thornicroft" title="Harry Scott Thornicroft">Harry Scott Thornicroft</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup> also known as <b>Thornicroft giraffe</b></td>
<td>The Rhodesian giraffe is an <a href="/wiki/Ecotype" title="Ecotype">ecotype</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> (<i>G. tippelskirchi</i>) restricted to the <a href="/wiki/Luangwa_River" title="Luangwa River">Luangwa Valley</a> in eastern Zambia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-12"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> The patches are notched and somewhat star-shaped, and may or may not extend across the legs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-13"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> The median lump of males is underdeveloped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:54</sup> No more than 550 remain in the wild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-11"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup> with none kept in zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISIS_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-24">[24]</a></sup> According to the 2016 genetic analysis, it is synonymous with the <a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> (<i>G. tippelskirchi</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cur.biol_2016_23-12"><a href="#cite_note-cur.biol_2016-23">[23]</a></sup></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_camelopardalis_thornicrofti.jpg"><img alt="Giraffa camelopardalis thornicrofti.jpg" data-file-height="4352" data-file-width="3264" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Giraffa_camelopardalis_thornicrofti.jpg/150px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_thornicrofti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Giraffa_camelopardalis_thornicrofti.jpg/225px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_thornicrofti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Giraffa_camelopardalis_thornicrofti.jpg/300px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_thornicrofti.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>There are also seven extinct species of giraffe, listed as the following-</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a><i>Giraffa gracilis</i></li>
<li>†<i><a href="/wiki/Giraffa_jumae" title="Giraffa jumae">Giraffa jumae</a></i></li>
<li>†<i>Giraffa priscilla</i></li>
<li>†<i>Giraffa punjabiensis</i></li>
<li>†<i>Giraffa pygmaea</i></li>
<li>†<i>Giraffa sivalensis</i></li>
<li>†<i>Giraffa stillei</i></li>
</ul>
<p><i>G. attica</i>, also extinct, was formerly considered part of <i>Giraffa</i> but was reclassified as <i><a href="/wiki/Bohlinia" title="Bohlinia">Bohlinia attica</a></i> in 1929.<sup><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Giraffe#cite_note-Mitchell20003-5" title="Northern Giraffe">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance_and_anatomy">Appearance and anatomy</span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Giraffa_camelopardalis_anatomy" title="commons:Category:Giraffa camelopardalis anatomy">Giraffa camelopardalis anatomy</a></b></i>.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe08_-_melbourne_zoo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Giraffe08_-_melbourne_zoo.jpg/220px-Giraffe08_-_melbourne_zoo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Giraffe08_-_melbourne_zoo.jpg/330px-Giraffe08_-_melbourne_zoo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Giraffe08_-_melbourne_zoo.jpg/440px-Giraffe08_-_melbourne_zoo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

Closeup of the head of a giraffe at the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Zoo" title="Melbourne Zoo">Melbourne Zoo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_skeleton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="595" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Giraffe_skeleton.jpg/220px-Giraffe_skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Giraffe_skeleton.jpg/330px-Giraffe_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Giraffe_skeleton.jpg/440px-Giraffe_skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

Giraffe skeleton on display at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Osteology" title="Museum of Osteology">Museum of Osteology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City,_Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma City, Oklahoma">Oklahoma City, Oklahoma</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="627" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg/150px-Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg/225px-Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg/300px-Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masai_Giraffe_right-rear_foot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Right hind leg of a Masai giraffe at <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Zoo" title="San Diego Zoo">San Diego Zoo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fully grown giraffes stand 4.3–5.7 m (14.1–18.7 ft) tall, with males taller than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nowak1999_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak1999-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OwenSmith1988_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-OwenSmith1988-38">[38]</a></sup> The tallest recorded male was 5.88 m (19.3 ft) and the tallest recorded female was 5.17 m (17.0 ft) tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nowak1999_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak1999-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg-39">[39]</a></sup> The average weight is 1,192 kg (2,628 lb) for an adult male and 828 kg (1,825 lb) for an adult female<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skinner1990_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skinner1990-40">[40]</a></sup> with maximum weights of 1,930 kg (4,250 lb) and 1,180 kg (2,600 lb) having been recorded for males and females, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OwenSmith1988_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-OwenSmith1988-38">[38]</a></sup> Despite its long neck and legs, the giraffe's body is relatively short.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:66</sup> Located at both sides of the head, the giraffe's large, bulging eyes give it good all-round vision from its great height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:25</sup> Giraffes see in colour<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:26</sup> and their senses of hearing and <a href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">smell</a> are also sharp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup> The animal can close its muscular nostrils to protect against sandstorms and ants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27</sup></p>
<p>The giraffe's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehensile" title="Prehensile">prehensile</a> tongue is about 45 cm (18 in) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OwenSmith1988_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-OwenSmith1988-38">[38]</a></sup> It is purplish-black in colour, perhaps to protect against sunburn, and is useful for grasping foliage, as well as for grooming and cleaning the animal's nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27</sup> The upper lip of the giraffe is also prehensile and useful when foraging. The lips, tongue, and inside of the mouth are covered in <a href="/wiki/Taste_bud" title="Taste bud">papillae</a> to protect against thorns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Giraffa_camelopardalis_reticulata-atTobuZoo-2012.ogv/854px--Giraffa_camelopardalis_reticulata-atTobuZoo-2012.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="24.457766666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Giraffa_camelopardalis_reticulata-atTobuZoo-2012.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Giraffa_camelopardalis_reticulata-atTobuZoo-2012.ogv/Giraffa_camelopardalis_reticulata-atTobuZoo-2012.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm;
