<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_camelopardalis_reticulata-atTobuZoo-2012.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
(video) A pair of giraffes at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tobu_Zoo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tobu Zoo (page does not exist)">Tobu Zoo</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saitama_prefecture" title="Saitama prefecture">Saitama</a>, Japan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The coat has dark blotches or patches (which can be orange, <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_(color)" title="Chestnut (color)">chestnut</a>, brown, or nearly black in colour<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup>) separated by light hair (usually white or <a href="/wiki/Cream_(colour)" title="Cream (colour)">cream</a> in colour<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup>). Male giraffes become darker as they age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> The coat pattern has been claimed to serve as <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a> in the light and shade patterns of savannah woodlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup> While adult giraffes standing among trees and bushes are hard to see at even a few metres' distance, they actively move into the open to gain the best view of an approaching predator, obviating any benefit that camouflage might bring. Instead, the adults rely on their size and ability to defend themselves. However, camouflage appears to be important for calves, which spend a large part of the day in hiding, away from their mothers; further, over half of all calves die within a year, so predation is certainly important. It appears, therefore, that the spotted coat of the giraffe functions as camouflage for the young, while adults simply inherit this coloration as a by-product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup> The skin underneath the dark areas may serve as windows for <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a>, being sites for complex blood vessel systems and large sweat glands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2004_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2004-43">[43]</a></sup> Each individual giraffe has a unique coat pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The skin of a giraffe is mostly gray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skinner1990_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Skinner1990-40">[40]</a></sup> Its thickness allows the animal to run through thorn bush without being punctured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:34</sup> The fur may serve as a chemical defence, as its parasite repellents give the animal a characteristic scent. At least 11 main <a href="/wiki/Aromaticity" title="Aromaticity">aromatic</a> chemicals are in the fur, although <a href="/wiki/Indole" title="Indole">indole</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-methylindole" title="3-methylindole">3-methylindole</a> are responsible for most of the smell. Because the males have a stronger odour than the females, the odour may also have sexual function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Along the animal's neck is a mane made of short, erect hairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> The one-metre (3.3-ft) tail ends in a long, dark tuft of hair and is used as a defense against insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:36</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skull_and_ossicones">Skull and ossicones</span></h3>
<p>Both sexes have prominent horn-like structures called ossicones, which are formed from ossified cartilage, covered in skin and fused to the skull at the <a href="/wiki/Parietal_bone" title="Parietal bone">parietal bones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> Being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular" title="Vascular">vascularized</a>, the ossicones may have a role in thermoregulation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2004_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2004-43">[43]</a></sup> and are also used in combat between males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim2010_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-sim2010-45">[45]</a></sup> Appearance is a reliable guide to the sex or age of a giraffe: the ossicones of females and young are thin and display tufts of hair on top, whereas those of adult males end in knobs and tend to be bald on top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> Also, a median lump, which is more prominent in males, emerges at the front of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> Males develop <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> deposits that form bumps on their skulls as they age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup> A giraffe's skull is lightened by multiple <a href="/wiki/Sinus_(anatomy)" title="Sinus (anatomy)">sinuses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:70</sup> However, as males age, their skulls become heavier and more club-like, helping them become more dominant in combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> The upper jaw has a grooved <a href="/wiki/Palate" title="Palate">palate</a> and lacks front teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:26</sup> The giraffe's <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molars</a> have a rough surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-7"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legs.2C_locomotion_and_posture">Legs, locomotion and posture</span></h3>
<p>The front and back legs of a giraffe are about the same length. The <a href="/wiki/Radius_(bone)" title="Radius (bone)">radius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulna" title="Ulna">ulna</a> of the front legs are articulated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpus" title="Carpus">carpus</a>, which, while structurally equivalent to the human wrist, functions as a knee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> It appears that a <a href="/wiki/Suspensory_ligament" title="Suspensory ligament">suspensory ligament</a> allows the lanky legs to support the animal's great weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The foot of the giraffe reaches a diametre of 30 cm (12 in), and the <a href="/wiki/Hoof" title="Hoof">hoof</a> is 15 cm (5.9 in) high in males and 10 cm (3.9 in) in females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-8"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:36</sup> The rear of each hoof is low and the <a href="/wiki/Fetlock" title="Fetlock">fetlock</a> is close to the ground, allowing the foot to provide additional support to the animal's weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> Giraffes lack <a href="/wiki/Dewclaw" title="Dewclaw">dewclaws</a> and interdigital glands. The giraffe's pelvis, though relatively short, has an <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a> that is outspread at the upper ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>A giraffe has only two <a href="/wiki/Gait" title="Gait">gaits</a>: walking and galloping. Walking is done by moving the legs on one side of the body at the same time, then doing the same on the other side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-6"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> When galloping, the hind legs move around the front legs before the latter move forward,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup> and the tail will curl up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-7"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> The animal relies on the forward and backward motions of its head and neck to maintain balance and the counter momentum while galloping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:327–29</sup> The giraffe can reach a sprint speed of up to 60 km/h (37 mph),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and can sustain 50 km/h (31 mph) for several kilometres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>A giraffe rests by lying with its body on top of its folded legs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:329</sup> To lie down, the animal kneels on its front legs and then lowers the rest of its body. To get back up, it first gets on its knees and spreads its hind legs to raise its hindquarters. It then straightens its front legs. With each step, the animal swings its head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-9"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:31</sup> In captivity, the giraffe sleeps intermittently around 4.6 hours per day, mostly at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sleep_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-sleep-50">[50]</a></sup> It usually sleeps lying down, however, standing sleeps have been recorded, particularly in older individuals. Intermittent short "deep sleep" phases while lying are characterised by the giraffe bending its neck backwards and resting its head on the hip or thigh, a position believed to indicate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paradoxical_sleep" title="Paradoxical sleep">paradoxical sleep</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sleep_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-sleep-50">[50]</a></sup> If the giraffe wants to bend down to drink, it either spreads its front legs or bends its knees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-8"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> Giraffes would probably not be competent swimmers as their long legs would be highly cumbersome in the water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swim_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-swim-51">[51]</a></sup> although they could possibly float.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naish_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naish-52">[52]</a></sup> When swimming, the thorax would be weighed down by the front legs, making it difficult for the animal to move its neck and legs in harmony<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swim_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-swim-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naish_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naish-52">[52]</a></sup> or keep its head above the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swim_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-swim-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neck">Neck</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="669" data-file-width="611" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png/200px-Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png/300px-Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png/400px-Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Okapi_Giraffe_Neck.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Despite the vast difference in neck length the giraffe (right) and the okapi (left) both have seven cervical vertebrae.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The giraffe has an extremely elongated neck, which can be up to 2–2.4 m (6.6–7.9 ft) in length, accounting for much of the animal's vertical height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OwenSmith1988_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-OwenSmith1988-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-10"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:29</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor.26Wedel2013_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor.26Wedel2013-53">[53]</a></sup> The long neck results from a disproportionate lengthening of the <a href="/wiki/Cervical_vertebrae" title="Cervical vertebrae">cervical vertebrae</a>, not from the addition of more vertebrae. Each cervical vertebra is over 28 cm (11 in) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:71</sup> They comprise 52–54 per cent of the length of the giraffe's <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a>, compared with the 27–33 percent typical of similar large ungulates, including the giraffe’s closest living relative, the <a href="/wiki/Okapi" title="Okapi">okapi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bada_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-bada-17">[17]</a></sup> This elongation largely takes place after birth, as giraffe mothers would have a difficult time giving birth to young with the same neck proportions as adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-van-54">[54]</a></sup> The giraffe's head and neck are held up by large muscles and a strengthened <a href="/wiki/Nuchal_ligament" title="Nuchal ligament">nuchal ligament</a>, which are anchored by long dorsal spines on the anterior <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_vertebrae" title="Thoracic vertebrae">thoracic vertebrae</a>, giving the animal a hump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solounias1999_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solounias1999-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1001" data-file-width="600" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg/200px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg/300px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg/400px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_High-rise_living.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An adult male <a href="/wiki/Reticulated_giraffe" title="Reticulated giraffe">reticulated giraffe</a> feeding high up on an acacia, in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The giraffe's neck vertebrae have <a href="/wiki/Ball_and_socket_joint" title="Ball and socket joint">ball and socket joints</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:71</sup> In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(anatomy)" title="Atlas (anatomy)">atlas</a>–<a href="/wiki/Axis_(anatomy)" title="Axis (anatomy)">axis</a> joint (C1 and C2) allows the animal to tilt its head vertically and reach more branches with the tongue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-11"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:29</sup> The point of articulation between the cervical and thoracic vertebrae of giraffes is shifted to lie between the first and second thoracic vertebrae (T1 and T2), unlike most other ruminants where the articulation is between the seventh cervical vertebra (C7) and T1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bada_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-bada-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-van-54">[54]</a></sup> This allows C7 to contribute directly to increased neck length and has given rise to the suggestion that T1 is actually C8, and that giraffes have added an extra cervical vertebra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solounias1999_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Solounias1999-55">[55]</a></sup> However, this proposition is not generally accepted, as T1 has other morphological features, such as an articulating <a href="/wiki/Rib" title="Rib">rib</a>, deemed diagnostic of thoracic vertebrae, and because exceptions to the mammalian limit of seven cervical vertebrae are generally characterised by increased <a href="/wiki/Neurological_disorder" title="Neurological disorder">neurological anomalies</a> and maladies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bada_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-bada-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are several hypotheses regarding the evolutionary origin and maintenance of elongation in giraffe necks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim2010_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-sim2010-45">[45]</a></sup> The "competing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Browsing_(predation)" title="Browsing (predation)">browsers</a> hypothesis" was originally suggested by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> and challenged only recently. It suggests that competitive pressure from smaller browsers, such as <a href="/wiki/Kudu" title="Kudu">kudu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steenbok" title="Steenbok">steenbok</a> and <a href="/wiki/Impala" title="Impala">impala</a>, encouraged the elongation of the neck, as it enabled giraffes to reach food that competitors could not. This advantage is real, as giraffes can and do feed up to 4.5 m (15 ft) high, while even quite large competitors, such as kudu, can feed up to only about 2 m (6 ft 7 in) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dt1990_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-dt1990-56">[56]</a></sup> There is also research suggesting that browsing competition is intense at lower levels, and giraffes feed more efficiently (gaining more leaf biomass with each mouthful) high in the canopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> However, scientists disagree about just how much time giraffes spend feeding at levels beyond the reach of other browsers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim2010_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-sim2010-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dt1990_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-dt1990-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sexdiff_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-sexdiff-59">[59]</a></sup> and a 2010 study found that adult giraffes with longer necks actually suffered higher mortality rates under drought conditions than their shorter-necked counterparts. This study suggests that maintaining a longer neck requires more nutrients, which puts longer-necked giraffes at risk during a food shortage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demography_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-demography-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another theory, the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a> hypothesis, proposes that the long necks evolved as a secondary <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual characteristic</a>, giving males an advantage in "necking" contests (see below) to establish dominance and obtain access to sexually receptive females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup> In support of this theory, necks are longer and heavier for males than females of the same age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim2010_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-sim2010-45">[45]</a></sup> and the former do not employ other forms of combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup> However, one objection is that it fails to explain why female giraffes also have long necks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> It has also been proposed that the neck serves to give the animal greater vigilance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_systems">Internal systems</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="900" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Rainbirder_-_Reticulated_Giraffe_drinking.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Reticulated_giraffe" title="Reticulated giraffe">Reticulated giraffe</a> bending down to drink, in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>. The circulatory system is adapted to deal with blood flow rushing down its neck.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In mammals, the left <a href="/wiki/Recurrent_laryngeal_nerve" title="Recurrent laryngeal nerve">recurrent laryngeal nerve</a> is longer than the right; in the giraffe it is over 30 cm (12 in) longer. These nerves are longer in the giraffe than in any other living animal;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wedel_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wedel-64">[64]</a></sup> the left nerve is over 2 m (6 ft 7 in) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Each nerve cell in this path begins in the <a href="/wiki/Brainstem" title="Brainstem">brainstem</a> and passes down the neck along the <a href="/wiki/Vagus_nerve" title="Vagus nerve">vagus nerve</a>, then branches off into the recurrent laryngeal nerve which passes back up the neck to the larynx. Thus, these nerve cells have a length of nearly 5 m (16 ft) in the largest giraffes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wedel_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wedel-64">[64]</a></sup> The structure of a giraffe's brain resembles that of domestic cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-12"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:31</sup> It is kept cool by evaporative heat loss in the nasal passages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2004_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2004-43">[43]</a></sup> The shape of the skeleton gives the giraffe a small lung volume relative to its mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SkinnerJD2011_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-SkinnerJD2011-66">[66]</a></sup> Its long neck gives it a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Dead_space_(physiology)" title="Dead space (physiology)">dead space</a>, in spite of its narrow windpipe. These factors increase the resistance to airflow. Nevertheless, the animal can still supply enough oxygen to its tissues and it can increase its respiratory rate and oxygen diffusion when running.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SkinnerJD2011_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-SkinnerJD2011-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3341" data-file-width="4170" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG/220px-Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG/330px-Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG/440px-Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe-Drinking-Nairobi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Giraffe's snout while drinking</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> of the giraffe has several adaptations for its great height. Its heart, which can weigh more than 11 kg (25 lb) and measures about 60 cm (2 ft) long, must generate approximately double the blood pressure required for a human to maintain blood flow to the brain. As such, the wall of the heart can be as thick as 7.5 cm (3.0 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup> Giraffes have unusually high heart rates for their size, at 150 beats per minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:76</sup> When the animal lowers its head the blood rushes down fairly unopposed and a <a href="/wiki/Rete_mirabile" title="Rete mirabile">rete mirabile</a> in the upper neck prevents excess blood flow to the brain. When it raises again, the blood vessels constrict and direct the blood into the brain so the animal doesn't faint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jugular_vein" title="Jugular vein">jugular veins</a> contain several (most commonly seven) valves to prevent blood flowing back into the head from the <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior vena cava</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_atrium" title="Right atrium">right atrium</a> while the head is lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Conversely, the blood vessels in the lower legs are under great pressure because of the weight of fluid pressing down on them. To solve this problem, the skin of the lower legs is thick and tight; preventing too much blood from pouring into them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Giraffes have <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">oesophageal muscles</a> that are unusually strong to allow regurgitation of food from the stomach up the neck and into the mouth for <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ruminate" title="wikt:ruminate">rumination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-5"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:78</sup> They have four chambered stomachs, as in all ruminants, and the first chamber has adapted to their specialised diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> The intestines of an adult giraffe measure more than 70 m (230 ft) in length<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PerezEtAl2009_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-PerezEtAl2009-69">[69]</a></sup> and have a relatively small ratio of small to large intestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PerezEtAl2009_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-PerezEtAl2009-69">[69]</a></sup> The liver of the giraffe is small and compact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-6"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:76</sup> A gallbladder is generally present during fetal life, but it may disappear before birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour_and_ecology">Behaviour and ecology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_and_feeding">Habitat and feeding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_feeding,_Tanzania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Giraffe_feeding%2C_Tanzania.jpg/220px-Giraffe_feeding%2C_Tanzania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Giraffe_feeding%2C_Tanzania.jpg/330px-Giraffe_feeding%2C_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Giraffe_feeding%2C_Tanzania.jpg/440px-Giraffe_feeding%2C_Tanzania.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_feeding,_Tanzania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> extending its tongue to feed, in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>. Its tongue, lips and palate are tough enough to deal with sharp thorns in trees.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Giraffes usually inhabit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">savannahs</a> and open <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a>. They prefer <a href="/wiki/Acacieae" title="Acacieae">Acacieae</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Commiphora" title="Commiphora">Commiphora</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Combretum" title="Combretum">Combretum</a></i> and open <i><a href="/wiki/Terminalia_(plant)" title="Terminalia (plant)">Terminalia</a></i> woodlands over denser environments like <i><a href="/wiki/Brachystegia" title="Brachystegia">Brachystegia</a></i> woodlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:322</sup> The Angolan giraffe can be found in desert environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Giraffes browse on the twigs of trees, preferring trees of the subfamily Acacieae and the genera <i>Commiphora</i> and <i>Terminalia</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-4">[4]</a></sup> which are important sources of calcium and protein to sustain the giraffe's growth rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell20003_10-10"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell20003-10">[10]</a></sup> They also feed on shrubs, grass and fruit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:324</sup> A giraffe eats around 34 kg (75 lb) of foliage daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-9"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> When stressed, giraffes may chew the bark off branches. Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbivorous" title="Herbivorous">herbivorous</a>, the giraffe has been known to visit carcasses and lick dried meat off bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:325</sup></p>
<p>During the wet season, food is abundant and giraffes are more spread out, while during the dry season, they gather around the remaining evergreen trees and bushes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-4">[4]</a></sup> Mothers tend to feed in open areas, presumably to make it easier to detect predators, although this may reduce their feeding efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sexdiff_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-sexdiff-59">[59]</a></sup> As a <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminant</a>, the giraffe first chews its food, then swallows it for processing and then visibly passes the half-digested cud up the neck and back into the mouth to chew again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-7"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:78–79</sup> It is common for a giraffe to salivate while feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-13"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27</sup> The giraffe requires less food than many other herbivores because the foliage it eats has more concentrated nutrients and it has a more efficient digestive system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-4">[4]</a></sup> The animal's faeces come in the form of small pellets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> When it has access to water, a giraffe drinks at intervals no longer than three days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-10"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Giraffes have a great effect on the trees that they feed on, delaying the growth of young trees for some years and giving "waistlines" to trees that are too tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-11"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> Feeding is at its highest during the first and last hours of daytime. Between these hours, giraffes mostly stand and ruminate. Rumination is the dominant activity during the night, when it is mostly done lying down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-12"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_life">Social life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_(Giraffa_camelopardalis)_females.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3554" data-file-width="5332" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Giraffe_%28Giraffa_camelopardalis%29_females.jpg/220px-Giraffe_%28Giraffa_camelopardalis%29_females.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Giraffe_%28Giraffa_camelopardalis%29_females.jpg/330px-Giraffe_%28Giraffa_camelopardalis%29_females.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Giraffe_%28Giraffa_camelopardalis%29_females.jpg/440px-Giraffe_%28Giraffa_camelopardalis%29_females.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_(Giraffa_camelopardalis)_females.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gathering of female <a href="/wiki/South_African_giraffe" title="South African giraffe">southern giraffes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tswalu_Kalahari_Reserve" title="Tswalu Kalahari Reserve">Tswalu Kalahari Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. These animals commonly gather in herds.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Giraffes are usually found in groups. Traditionally, the composition of these groups has been described as open and ever-changing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manyara_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manyara-73">[73]</a></sup> Giraffes were thought to have few social bonds and for research purposes, a "group" has been defined as "a collection of individuals that are less than a kilometre apart and moving in the same general direction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratt_1985_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1985-74">[74]</a></sup> More recent studies have found that giraffes do have long-term social associations and may form groups or pairs based on kinship, sex or other factors. These groups may regularly associate with one another in larger communities or sub-communities within a <a href="/wiki/Fission%E2%80%93fusion_society" title="Fission–fusion society">fission–fusion society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bercovitch_2013_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bercovitch_2013-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanderWaal_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanderWaal-77">[77]</a></sup> The number of giraffes in a group can range up to 44 individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanderWaal_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-VanderWaal-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">

</tr>
</table>
<p>Giraffe groups tend to be sex-segregated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanderWaal_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-VanderWaal-77">[77]</a></sup> although mixed-sex groups made of adult females and young males are known to occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratt_1985_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1985-74">[74]</a></sup> Particularity stable giraffe groups are those made of mothers and their young,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratt_1985_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1985-74">[74]</a></sup> which can last weeks or months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leuthold_1979_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leuthold_1979-78">[78]</a></sup> Social cohesion in these groups is maintained by the bonds formed between calves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:330</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratt_1985_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1985-74">[74]</a></sup> Female association appears to be based on <a href="/wiki/Philopatry" title="Philopatry">space-use</a> and individuals may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrilineally" title="Matrilineally">matrilineally</a> related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanderWaal_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-VanderWaal-77">[77]</a></sup> In general, females are more selective than males in who they associate with in regards to individuals of the same sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-76">[76]</a></sup> Young males also form groups and will engage in playfights. However, as they get older males become more solitary but may also associate in pairs or with female groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanderWaal_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-VanderWaal-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leuthold_1979_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leuthold_1979-78">[78]</a></sup> Giraffes are not <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorial</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> but they have <a href="/wiki/Home_range" title="Home range">home ranges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-13"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> Male giraffes occasionally wander far from areas that they normally frequent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:329</sup></p>
<p>Although generally quiet and non-vocal, giraffes have been heard to communicate using various sounds. During courtship, males emit loud coughs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-14"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> Females call their young by bellowing. Calves will emit snorts, bleats, mooing and mewing sounds. Giraffes also snore, hiss, moan, grunt and make flute-like sounds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-15"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hum_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-hum-79">[79]</a></sup> and possibly communicate over long distances using <a href="/wiki/Infrasound" title="Infrasound">infrasound</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>—though this is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hum_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-hum-79">[79]</a></sup> During nighttime, giraffes appear to <a href="/wiki/Humming" title="Humming">hum</a> to each other above the infrasound range for purposes which are unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hum_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-hum-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_parental_care">Reproduction and parental care</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_(courting).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3458" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28courting%29.jpg/200px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28courting%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28courting%29.jpg/300px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28courting%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28courting%29.jpg/400px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28courting%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_(mating).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2415" data-file-width="1811" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28mating%29.jpg/200px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28mating%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28mating%29.jpg/300px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28mating%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28mating%29.jpg/400px-Giraffa_camelopardalis_angolensis_%28mating%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Angolan_giraffe" title="Angolan giraffe">Southern giraffes</a> courting (above) and mating in <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>. Generally, only dominant males are able to mate with females.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reproduction in giraffes is broadly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polygamous" title="Polygamous">polygamous</a>: a few older males mate with the fertile females. Male giraffes assess female fertility by tasting the female's urine to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oestrus" title="Oestrus">oestrus</a>, in a multi-step process known as the <a href="/wiki/Flehmen_response" title="Flehmen response">flehmen response</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratt_1985_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1985-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leuthold_1979_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leuthold_1979-78">[78]</a></sup> Males prefer young adult females over juveniles and older adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratt_1985_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1985-74">[74]</a></sup> Once an oestrous female is detected, the male will attempt to court her. When courting, dominant males will keep subordinate ones at bay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leuthold_1979_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-Leuthold_1979-78">[78]</a></sup> A courting male may lick a female's tail, rest his head and neck on her body or nudge her with his horns. During copulation, the male stands on his hind legs with his head held up and his front legs resting on the female's sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-16"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Giraffe <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a> lasts 400–460 days, after which a single calf is normally born, although twins occur on rare occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap-81">[81]</a></sup> The mother gives birth standing up. The calf emerges head and front legs first, having broken through the <a href="/wiki/Fetal_membrane" title="Fetal membrane">fetal membranes</a>, and falls to the ground, severing the <a href="/wiki/Umbilical_cord" title="Umbilical cord">umbilical cord</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> The mother then grooms the newborn and helps it stand up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-14"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:40</sup> A newborn giraffe is 1.7–2 m (5.6–6.6 ft) tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nowak1999_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak1999-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OwenSmith1988_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-OwenSmith1988-38">[38]</a></sup> Within a few hours of birth, the calf can run around and is almost indistinguishable from a one-week-old. However, for the first 1–3 weeks, it spends most of its time hiding;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langman_1977_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langman_1977-82">[82]</a></sup> its coat pattern providing camouflage. The ossicones, which have lain flat while it was in the womb, become erect within a few days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-17"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_Family.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2167" data-file-width="3250" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Giraffe_Family.jpg/220px-Giraffe_Family.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Giraffe_Family.jpg/330px-Giraffe_Family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Giraffe_Family.jpg/440px-Giraffe_Family.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_Family.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mother <a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> and calves feeding in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>. It is mostly the females that raise young, and they may gather in nursery herds.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mothers with calves will gather in nursery herds, moving or browsing together. Mothers in such a group may sometimes leave their calves with one female while they forage and drink elsewhere. This is known as a "<a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A8che_(zoology)" title="Crèche (zoology)">calving pool</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langman_1977_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Langman_1977-82">[82]</a></sup> Adult males play almost no role in raising the young,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:337</sup> although they appear to have friendly interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pratt_1985_74-6"><a href="#cite_note-Pratt_1985-74">[74]</a></sup> Calves are at risk of predation, and a mother giraffe will stand over her calf and kick at an approaching predator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-18"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> Females watching calving pools will only alert their own young if they detect a disturbance, although the others will take notice and follow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langman_1977_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Langman_1977-82">[82]</a></sup> The bond a mother shares with her calf varies, though it can last until her next calving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langman_1977_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-Langman_1977-82">[82]</a></sup> Likewise, calves may suckle for only a month<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:335</sup> or as long as a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-19"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leuthold_1979_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-Leuthold_1979-78">[78]</a></sup> Females become sexually mature when they are four years old, while males become mature at four or five years. However, males must wait until they are at least seven years old to gain the opportunity to mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-20"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-15"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:40</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Necking">Necking</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG/220px-Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG/330px-Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG/440px-Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_Ithala_KZN_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Here, male <a href="/wiki/South_African_giraffe" title="South African giraffe">southern giraffes</a> engage in low intensity necking to establish dominance, in <a href="/wiki/Ithala_Game_Reserve" title="Ithala Game Reserve">Ithala Game Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">Kwa-Zulu-Natal</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="3965" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG/220px-Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG/330px-Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG/440px-Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe-Necking-Etosha.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The more active kind of necking, engaged with here by <a href="/wiki/Angolan_giraffe" title="Angolan giraffe">southern giraffes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Male giraffes use their necks as weapons in combat, a behaviour known as "necking". Necking is used to establish dominance and males that win necking bouts have greater <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_success" title="Reproductive success">reproductive success</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup> This behaviour occurs at low or high intensity. In low intensity necking, the combatants rub and lean against each other. The male that can hold itself more erect wins the bout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-21"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> In high intensity necking, the combatants will spread their front legs and swing their necks at each other, attempting to land blows with their ossicones. The contestants will try to dodge each other's blows and then get ready to counter. The power of a blow depends on the weight of the skull and the arc of the swing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-22"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> A necking duel can last more than half an hour, depending on how well matched the combatants are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-10"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:331</sup> Although most fights do not lead to serious injury, there have been records of broken jaws, broken necks, and even deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a duel, it is common for two male giraffes to caress and court each other. Such interactions between males have been found to be more frequent than heterosexual coupling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In one study, up to 94 percent of observed mounting incidents took place between males. The proportion of same-sex activities varied from 30–75 percent. Only one percent of same-sex mounting incidents occurred between females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mortality_and_health">Mortality and health</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lioness_with_giraffe_kill,_jackal_lurking,_kenya,_august_9th_2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="2048" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Lioness_with_giraffe_kill%2C_jackal_lurking%2C_kenya%2C_august_9th_2012.jpg/220px-Lioness_with_giraffe_kill%2C_jackal_lurking%2C_kenya%2C_august_9th_2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Lioness_with_giraffe_kill%2C_jackal_lurking%2C_kenya%2C_august_9th_2012.jpg/330px-Lioness_with_giraffe_kill%2C_jackal_lurking%2C_kenya%2C_august_9th_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Lioness_with_giraffe_kill%2C_jackal_lurking%2C_kenya%2C_august_9th_2012.jpg/440px-Lioness_with_giraffe_kill%2C_jackal_lurking%2C_kenya%2C_august_9th_2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lioness_with_giraffe_kill,_jackal_lurking,_kenya,_august_9th_2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">Lioness</a> seen with an adult <a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> kill</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Giraffes have high adult survival probability,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-85">[85]</a></sup> and an unusually long lifespan compared to other ruminants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> up to 25 years in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup> Because of their size, eyesight and powerful kicks, adult giraffes are usually not subject to predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_35-23"><a href="#cite_note-estes-35">[35]</a></sup> However, they can fall prey to <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lions</a> and are regular prey for them in <a href="/wiki/Kruger_National_Park" title="Kruger National Park">Kruger National Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodiles</a> can also be a threat to giraffes when they bend down to drink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-16"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup> Calves are much more vulnerable than adults, and are additionally preyed on by <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">spotted hyenas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycaon_pictus" title="Lycaon pictus">wild dogs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup> A quarter to a half of giraffe calves reach adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The local, seasonal presence of large herds of migratory <a href="/wiki/Wildebeest" title="Wildebeest">wildebeests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebras</a> reduces predation pressure on giraffe calves and increases their survival probability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some parasites feed on giraffes. They are often hosts for <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a>, especially in the area around the genitals, which has thinner skin than other areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> Tick species that commonly feed on giraffes are those of genera <i><a href="/wiki/Hyalomma" title="Hyalomma">Hyalomma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Amblyomma" title="Amblyomma">Amblyomma</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rhipicephalus" title="Rhipicephalus">Rhipicephalus</a></i>. Giraffes may rely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red-billed_Oxpecker" title="Red-billed Oxpecker">red-billed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_oxpecker" title="Yellow-billed oxpecker">yellow-billed oxpeckers</a> to clean them of ticks and alert them to danger. Giraffes host numerous species of internal parasite and are susceptible to various diseases. They were victims of the (now eradicated) viral illness <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">rinderpest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> Giraffes also suffer from several disorders of the skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_cave_art.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="757" data-file-width="1000" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Giraffe_cave_art.jpg/220px-Giraffe_cave_art.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Giraffe_cave_art.jpg/330px-Giraffe_cave_art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Giraffe_cave_art.jpg/440px-Giraffe_cave_art.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giraffe_cave_art.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/San_rock_art" title="San rock art">San rock art</a> in Namibia depicting a giraffe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Humans have interacted with giraffes for millennia. The <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San people</a> of southern Africa have medicine dances named after some animals; the giraffe dance is performed to treat head ailments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> How the giraffe got its height has been the subject of various African <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folktales</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sim1996_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-sim1996-14">[14]</a></sup> including one from eastern Africa which explains that the giraffe grew tall from eating too many magic herbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Giraffes were depicted in art throughout the African continent, including that of the <a href="/wiki/Kiffian_culture" title="Kiffian culture">Kiffians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mero%C3%AB" title="Meroë">Meroë</a> <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-17"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:45–47</sup> The Kiffians were responsible for a life-size rock engraving of two giraffes that has been called the "world's largest rock art petroglyph".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-18"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:45</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The Egyptians gave the giraffe its own <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">hieroglyph</a>, named 'sr' in <a href="/wiki/Old_Egyptian_language" title="Old Egyptian language">Old Egyptian</a> and 'mmy' in later periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-19"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:49</sup> They also kept giraffes as pets and shipped them around the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-20"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:48–49</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="759" height="435" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/220px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/330px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/440px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Painting of a giraffe imported to China during the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The giraffe was also known to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, who believed that it was an unnatural hybrid of a <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a> and a leopard and called it <i>camelopardalis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-21"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:50</sup> The giraffe was among the many animals collected and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosaic_Fragment_with_Man_Leading_a_Giraffe_(Art_Institute_of_Chicago)" title="Mosaic Fragment with Man Leading a Giraffe (Art Institute of Chicago)">displayed</a> by the Romans. The first one in Rome was brought in by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> in 46 BC and exhibited to the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-22"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:52</sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a>, the housing of giraffes in Europe declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-23"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:54</sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, giraffes were known to Europeans through contact with the Arabs, who revered the giraffe for its peculiar appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero_2003_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero_2003-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Individual captive giraffes were given celebrity status throughout history. In 1414, a giraffe was shipped from <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>. It was then <a href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">taken to China</a> by explorer <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> and placed in a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> zoo. The animal was a source of fascination for the Chinese people, who associated it with the mythical <a href="/wiki/Qilin" title="Qilin">Qilin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-24"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:56</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Medici_giraffe" title="Medici giraffe">Medici giraffe</a> was a giraffe presented to <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici" title="Lorenzo de' Medici">Lorenzo de' Medici</a> in 1486. It caused a great stir on its arrival in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Another <a href="/wiki/Zarafa_(giraffe)" title="Zarafa (giraffe)">famous giraffe</a> was brought from <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt_under_the_Muhammad_Ali_dynasty" title="History of Egypt under the Muhammad Ali dynasty">Egypt</a> to Paris in the early 19th century as a gift from <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali of Egypt</a> to <a href="/wiki/Charles_X_of_France" title="Charles X of France">Charles X of France</a>. A sensation, the giraffe was the subject of numerous memorabilia or "giraffanalia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-25"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:81</sup></p>
<p>Giraffes continue to have a presence in modern culture. <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD" title="Salvador Dalí">Salvador Dalí</a> depicted them with burning manes in some of his surrealist paintings. Dali considered the giraffe to be a symbol of masculinity, and a flaming giraffe was meant to be a "masculine cosmic apocalyptic monster".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-26"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:123</sup> Several children's books feature the giraffe, including David A. Ufer's <i>The Giraffe Who Was Afraid of Heights</i>, <a href="/wiki/Giles_Andreae" title="Giles Andreae">Giles Andreae</a>'s <i>Giraffes Can't Dance</i> and <a href="/wiki/Roald_Dahl" title="Roald Dahl">Roald Dahl</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Giraffe_and_the_Pelly_and_Me" title="The Giraffe and the Pelly and Me">The Giraffe and the Pelly and Me</a></i>. Giraffes have appeared in animated films, as minor characters in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disney" title="Disney">Disney</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King" title="The Lion King">The Lion King</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dumbo" title="Dumbo">Dumbo</a></i>, and in more prominent roles in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wild" title="The Wild">The Wild</a></i> and in the <i><a href="/wiki/Madagascar_(franchise)" title="Madagascar (franchise)">Madagascar</a></i> films. <a href="/wiki/Sophie_the_Giraffe" title="Sophie the Giraffe">Sophie the Giraffe</a> has been a popular <a href="/wiki/Teether" title="Teether">teether</a> since 1961. Another famous fictional giraffe is the Toys "R" Us mascot <a href="/wiki/Toys_%22R%22_Us#Geoffrey_the_Giraffe" title='Toys "R" Us'>Geoffrey the Giraffe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-27"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:127</sup></p>
<p>The giraffe has also been used for some scientific experiments and discoveries. Scientists have looked at the properties of giraffe skin when developing suits for <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fighter_pilot" title="Fighter pilot">fighter pilots</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anatomy_41-8"><a href="#cite_note-anatomy-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:76</sup> because the people in these professions are in danger of passing out if blood rushes to their legs. Computer scientists have modeled the coat patterns of several subspecies using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction%E2%80%93diffusion" title="Reaction–diffusion">reaction–diffusion</a> mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Camelopardalis" title="Camelopardalis">Camelopardalis</a>, introduced in the seventeenth century, depicts a giraffe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-28"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:119–20</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tswana_people" title="Tswana people">Tswana people</a> of Botswana saw the constellation <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a> as two giraffes – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acrux" title="Acrux">Acrux</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_Crucis" title="Beta Crucis">Mimosa</a> forming a male, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gacrux" title="Gacrux">Gacrux</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delta_Crucis" title="Delta Crucis">Delta Crucis</a> forming the female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploitation_and_conservation_status">Exploitation and conservation status</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Giraffe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="784" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Giraffe.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Giraffe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Giraffe.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Giraffe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Giraffe.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Giraffe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0377,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Giraffe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Masai_giraffe" title="Masai giraffe">Masai giraffe</a> killed by tribesmen in <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> during the early 20th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Giraffes were probably common targets for hunters throughout Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-11"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup> Different parts of their bodies were used for different purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-14"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> Their meat was used for food. The tail hairs served as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flyswatter" title="Flyswatter">flyswatters</a>, bracelets, necklaces and thread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-15"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-12"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup> Shields, sandals and drums were made using the skin, and the strings of musical instruments were from the tendons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dagg1971_7-16"><a href="#cite_note-Dagg1971-7">[7]</a></sup> The smoke from burning giraffe skins was used by the medicine men of <a href="/wiki/Buganda" title="Buganda">Buganda</a> to treat nose bleeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kingdon1988_32-13"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon1988-32">[32]</a></sup> The Humr people of Sudan consume the drink Umm Nyolokh; which is created from the liver and marrow of giraffes. Umm Nyolokh often contains DMT and other psychoactive substances from plants the giraffes eat such as Acacia; and is known to cause hallucinations of giraffes, believed to be the giraffes' ghosts by the Humr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In the 19th century, European explorers began to hunt them for sport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_42-29"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-42">[42]</a></sup> Habitat destruction has hurt the giraffe, too: in the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>, the need for firewood and grazing room for livestock has led to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>. Normally, giraffes can coexist with livestock, since they do not directly compete with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, it was assessed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Least_Concern" title="Least Concern">Least Concern</a> from a conservation perspective by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN), but the 2016 assessment categorized giraffes as <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucn_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">[1]</a></sup> Giraffes have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extirpated" title="Extirpated">extirpated</a> from much of their historic range including <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>. They may also have disappeared from <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, but have been introduced to <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">Swaziland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucn_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">[1]</a></sup> Two subspecies, the <a href="/wiki/West_African_giraffe" title="West African giraffe">West African giraffe</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rothschild_giraffe" title="Rothschild giraffe">Rothschild giraffe</a>, have been classified as <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">Endangered</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNperalta_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNperalta-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNrothschildi_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNrothschildi-33">[33]</a></sup> as wild populations of each of them number in the hundreds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-14"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> In 1997, <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Kingdon" title="Jonathan Kingdon">Jonathan Kingdon</a> suggested that the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_giraffe" title="Nubian giraffe">Nubian giraffe</a> was the most threatened of all giraffes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-4">[4]</a></sup> as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Giraffe&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, it may number fewer than 250, although this estimate is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-15"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> Private game reserves have contributed to the preservation of giraffe populations in southern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Giraffe_Manor" title="Giraffe Manor">Giraffe Manor</a> is a popular hotel in <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a> that also serves as sanctuary for Rothschild's giraffes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The giraffe is a protected species in most of its range. It is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_animal" title="National animal">national animal</a> of Tanzania,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and is protected by law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FoleyFoley2014_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FoleyFoley2014-102">[102]</a></sup> Unauthorised killing can result in imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In 1999, it was estimated that over 140,000 giraffes existed in the wild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wildstatus_25-16"><a href="#cite_note-wildstatus-25">[25]</a></sup> estimations as of 2016 indicate that there are approximately 97,500 members of <i>Giraffa</i> in the wild, down from 155,000 in 1985, with around 1,144 in captivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>

<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="padding-left:0;padding-right:0;width:8.5em; background:#efefff;">

<li><a href="/wiki/Aders%27_duiker" title="Aders' duiker">Aders' duiker (<i>C. adersi</i>)</a></li>
